href="/wiki/Template_talk:French_literature_sidebar" title="Template talk:French literature sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:French_literature_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Jules Gabriel Verne</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="\'v\' in \'vie\'">v</span><span title="/&#604;&#720;r/: \'ur\' in \'fur\'">&#604;&#720;r</span><span title="\'n\' in \'nigh\'">n</span></span>/</a></span></span>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JCW_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-JCW-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> <small>French:&#160;</small><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/French" title="Help:IPA/French">[&#658;yl v&#603;&#641;n]</a></span>; 8 February 1828 &#8211; 24 March 1905) was a French novelist, poet, and playwright.\n</p><p>Jules Verne was born in the seaport of <a href="/wiki/Nantes" title="Nantes">Nantes</a>, he was trained to follow in his father\'s footsteps as a lawyer, but quit the profession early in life to write for magazines and the stage. His collaboration with the publisher <a href="/wiki/Pierre-Jules_Hetzel" title="Pierre-Jules Hetzel">Pierre-Jules Hetzel</a> led to the creation of the <i><a href="/wiki/Voyages_extraordinaires" title="Voyages extraordinaires">Voyages extraordinaires</a></i>, a widely popular series of scrupulously researched adventure novels including <i><a href="/wiki/Journey_to_the_Center_of_the_Earth" title="Journey to the Center of the Earth">Journey to the Center of the Earth</a></i> (1864), <i><a href="/wiki/Twenty_Thousand_Leagues_Under_the_Sea" title="Twenty Thousand Leagues Under the Sea">Twenty Thousand Leagues Under the Sea</a></i> (1870), and <i><a href="/wiki/Around_the_World_in_Eighty_Days" title="Around the World in Eighty Days">Around the World in Eighty Days</a></i> (1873).\n</p><p>Verne is generally considered a major literary author in France and most of Europe, where he has had a wide influence on the literary <a href="/wiki/Avant-garde" title="Avant-garde">avant-garde</a> and on <a href="/wiki/Surrealism" title="Surrealism">surrealism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAngenot197334_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAngenot197334-3">[3]</a></sup> His reputation is markedly different in <a href="/wiki/English-speaking_world" title="English-speaking world">Anglophone regions</a>, where he has often been labeled a writer of <a href="/wiki/Genre_fiction" title="Genre fiction">genre fiction</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Children%27s_books" title="Children\'s books">children\'s books</a>, largely because of the highly abridged and altered <a href="/wiki/Jules_Verne#English_translations" title="Jules Verne">translations</a> in which his novels are often reprinted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans200033_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans200033-4">[4]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Verne has been the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_most-translated_individual_authors" title="List of most-translated individual authors">second most-translated author in the world</a> since 1979, ranking between <a href="/wiki/Agatha_Christie" title="Agatha Christie">Agatha Christie</a> and <a href="/wiki/William_Shakespeare" title="William Shakespeare">William Shakespeare</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUNESCO2013_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUNESCO2013-5">[5]</a></sup> He has sometimes been called the "Father of Science Fiction", a title that has also been given to <a href="/wiki/H._G._Wells" title="H. G. Wells">H. G. Wells</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mary_Shelley" title="Mary Shelley">Mary Shelley</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hugo_Gernsback" title="Hugo Gernsback">Hugo Gernsback</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roberts48_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Roberts48-6">[6]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a href="#Life"><span class="tocnumber">1</span> <span class="toctext">Life</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-2"><a href="#Early_life"><span class="tocnumber">1.1</span> <span class="toctext">Early life</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-3"><a href="#Studies_in_Paris"><span class="tocnumber">1.2</span> <span class="toctext">Studies in Paris</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-4"><a href="#Literary_debut"><span class="tocnumber">1.3</span> <span class="toctext">Literary debut</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-5"><a href="#Family"><span class="tocnumber">1.4</span> <span class="toctext">Family</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-6"><a href="#Hetzel"><span class="tocnumber">1.5</span> <span class="toctext">Hetzel</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-7"><a href="#Later_years"><span class="tocnumber">1.6</span> <span class="toctext">Later years</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-8"><a href="#Death_and_posthumous_publications"><span class="tocnumber">1.7</span> <span class="toctext">Death and posthumous publications</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-9"><a href="#Works"><span class="tocnumber">2</span> <span class="toctext">Works</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-10"><a href="#Literary_reception"><span class="tocnumber">2.1</span> <span class="toctext">Literary reception</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-11"><a href="#English_translations"><span class="tocnumber">2.2</span> <span class="toctext">English translations</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-12"><a href="#Relationship_with_science_fiction"><span class="tocnumber">2.3</span> <span class="toctext">Relationship with science fiction</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-13"><a href="#Legacy"><span class="tocnumber">3</span> <span class="toctext">Legacy</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-14"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-15"><a href="#Footnotes"><span class="tocnumber">4.1</span> <span class="toctext">Footnotes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-16"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">4.2</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-17"><a href="#Citations"><span class="tocnumber">4.3</span> <span class="toctext">Citations</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-18"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Life">Life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jules_Verne&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jules_Verne&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:JMW_Turner_-_Nantes_from_the_Ile_Feydeau.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="613" data-file-width="900" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/JMW_Turner_-_Nantes_from_the_Ile_Feydeau.jpg/220px-JMW_Turner_-_Nantes_from_the_Ile_Feydeau.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/JMW_Turner_-_Nantes_from_the_Ile_Feydeau.jpg/330px-JMW_Turner_-_Nantes_from_the_Ile_Feydeau.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/JMW_Turner_-_Nantes_from_the_Ile_Feydeau.jpg/440px-JMW_Turner_-_Nantes_from_the_Ile_Feydeau.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:JMW_Turner_-_Nantes_from_the_Ile_Feydeau.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Nantes from &#206;le Feydeau, around the time of Verne\'s birth</div></div></div>\n<p>Jules Gabriel Verne was born on 8 February 1828, on &#206;le Feydeau, a small artificial island on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Loire_River" title="Loire River">Loire River</a> within the town of <a href="/wiki/Nantes" title="Nantes">Nantes</a>, in No. 4 Rue Olivier-de-Clisson, the house of his maternal grandmother Dame Sophie Allotte de la Fu&#255;e.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEButcher20065&#8211;6_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEButcher20065&#8211;6-7">[7]</a></sup> His parents were Pierre Verne, an attorney originally from <a href="/wiki/Provins" title="Provins">Provins</a>, and Sophie Allote de la Fu&#255;e, a Nantes woman from a local family of navigators and shipowners, of distant Scottish descent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEButcher2007_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEButcher2007-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fuye_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-fuye-9">[a]</a></sup> In 1829, the Verne family moved some hundred meters away to No. 2 Quai Jean-Bart, where Verne\'s brother Paul was born the same year. Three sisters, Anna (1836), Mathilde (1839), and Marie (1842) would follow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEButcher2007_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEButcher2007-8">[8]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1834, at the age of six, Verne was sent to boarding school at 5 Place du Bouffay in Nantes.  The teacher, Mme Sambin, was the widow of a naval captain who had disappeared some 30 years before.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJules-Verne19763_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJules-Verne19763-10">[9]</a></sup> Mme Sambin often told the students that her husband was a shipwrecked castaway and that he would eventually return like <a href="/wiki/Robinson_Crusoe" title="Robinson Crusoe">Robinson Crusoe</a> from his desert island paradise.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAllotte_de_la_Fu&#255;e195620_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAllotte_de_la_Fu&#255;e195620-11">[10]</a></sup> The theme of the <a href="/wiki/Robinsonade" title="Robinsonade">Robinsonade</a> would stay with Verne throughout his life and appear in many of his novels, including <i><a href="/wiki/The_Mysterious_Island" title="The Mysterious Island">The Mysterious Island</a></i> (1874), <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Second_Fatherland" title="Second Fatherland">Second Fatherland</a></i> (1900), and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_School_for_Robinsons" title="The School for Robinsons">The School for Robinsons</a></i> (1882).\n</p><p>In 1836, Verne went on to &#201;cole Saint&#8209;Stanislas, a Catholic school suiting the pious religious tastes of his father. Verne quickly distinguished himself in <i>m&#233;moire</i> (recitation from memory), geography, Greek, Latin, and singing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELottmann19969_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELottmann19969-12">[11]</a></sup> In the same year, 1836, Pierre Verne bought a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vacation_house" title="Vacation house">vacation house</a> at 29 Rue des R&#233;form&#233;s in the village of Chantenay (now part of Nantes) on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Loire_River" title="Loire River">Loire River</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETerres_d\'&#233;crivains2003_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETerres_d\'&#233;crivains2003-13">[12]</a></sup> In his brief memoir "Souvenirs d\'enfance et de jeunesse" ("Memories of Childhood and Youth", 1890), Verne recalled a deep fascination with the river and with the many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Merchant_vessel" title="Merchant vessel">merchant vessels</a> navigating it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVerne1890&#167;2_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVerne1890&#167;2-14">[13]</a></sup> He also took vacations at <a href="/wiki/Brains,_Loire-Atlantique" title="Brains, Loire-Atlantique">Brains</a>, in the house of his uncle Prudent Allotte, a retired shipowner, who had gone around the world and served as mayor of Brains from 1828 to 1837. Verne took joy in playing interminable rounds of the <a href="/wiki/Game_of_the_Goose" title="Game of the Goose">Game of the Goose</a> with his uncle, and both the game and his uncle\'s name would be memorialized in two late novels (<i><a href="/wiki/The_Will_of_an_Eccentric" title="The Will of an Eccentric">The Will of an Eccentric</a></i> (1900) and <i><a href="/wiki/Robur_the_Conqueror" title="Robur the Conqueror">Robur the Conqueror</a></i> (1886), respectively).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVerne1890&#167;2_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVerne1890&#167;2-14">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEComp&#232;re1997b35_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEComp&#232;re1997b35-15">[14]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Legend has it that in 1839, at the age of 11, Verne secretly procured a spot as <a href="/wiki/Cabin_boy" title="Cabin boy">cabin boy</a> on the three-mast ship <i>Coralie</i> with the intention of traveling to the Indies and bringing back a coral necklace for his cousin Caroline. The evening the ship set out for the Indies, it stopped first at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paimboeuf" title="Paimboeuf">Paimboeuf</a> where Pierre Verne arrived just in time to catch his son and make him promise to travel "only in his imagination".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAllotte_de_la_Fu&#255;e195626_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAllotte_de_la_Fu&#255;e195626-16">[15]</a></sup> It is now known that the legend is an exaggerated tale invented by Verne\'s first biographer, his niece Marguerite Allotte de la F&#252;ye, though it may have been inspired by a real incident.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEP&#233;rezde_VriesMargot2008[httpjvgileadorgilFAQC9_C9]_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEP&#233;rezde_VriesMargot2008[httpjvgileadorgilFAQC9_C9]-17">[16]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nantes_-_lyc%C3%A9e_Clemenceau.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2304" data-file-width="3072" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Nantes_-_lyc%C3%A9e_Clemenceau.jpg/220px-Nantes_-_lyc%C3%A9e_Clemenceau.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Nantes_-_lyc%C3%A9e_Clemenceau.jpg/330px-Nantes_-_lyc%C3%A9e_Clemenceau.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Nantes_-_lyc%C3%A9e_Clemenceau.jpg/440px-Nantes_-_lyc%C3%A9e_Clemenceau.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nantes_-_lyc%C3%A9e_Clemenceau.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Lyc&#233;e Royal in Nantes (now the Lyc&#233;e Georges-Clemenceau), where Jules Verne studied</div></div></div>\n<p>In 1840, the Vernes moved again to a large apartment at No. 6 Rue Jean-Jacques-Rousseau, where the family\'s youngest child, Marie, was born in 1842.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETerres_d\'&#233;crivains2003_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETerres_d\'&#233;crivains2003-13">[12]</a></sup> In the same year Verne entered another religious school, the Petit S&#233;minaire de Saint-Donatien, as a lay student. His unfinished novel <i>Un pr&#234;tre en 1839</i> (<i>A Priest in 1839</i>), written in his teens and the earliest of his prose works to survive,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELottmann199617_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELottmann199617-18">[17]</a></sup> describes the seminary in disparaging terms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELottmann19969_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELottmann19969-12">[11]</a></sup> From 1844 to 1846, Verne and his brother were enrolled in the Lyc&#233;e Royal (now the Lyc&#233;e Georges-Clemenceau) in Nantes. After finishing classes in rhetoric and philosophy, he took the <a href="/wiki/Baccalaur%C3%A9at" title="Baccalaur&#233;at">baccalaur&#233;at</a> at <a href="/wiki/Rennes" title="Rennes">Rennes</a> and received the grade "Fairly good" on 29 July 1846.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEComp&#232;re1997a20_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEComp&#232;re1997a20-19">[18]</a></sup>\n</p><p>By 1847, when Verne was 19, he had taken seriously to writing long works in the style of <a href="/wiki/Victor_Hugo" title="Victor Hugo">Victor Hugo</a>, beginning <i>Un pr&#234;tre en 1839</i> and seeing two verse tragedies, <i>Alexandre VI</i> and <i>La Conspiration des poudres</i> (<i>The Gunpowder Plot</i>), to completion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELottmann199617_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELottmann199617-18">[17]</a></sup> However, his father took it for granted that Verne, being the firstborn son of the family, would not attempt to make money in literature but would instead inherit the family law practice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELottmann199619_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELottmann199619-20">[19]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1847, Verne\'s father sent him to Paris, primarily to begin his studies in <a href="/wiki/Law_school" title="Law school">law school</a>, and secondarily (according to family legend) to distance him temporarily from Nantes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJules-Verne197610_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJules-Verne197610-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELottmann199614_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELottmann199614-22">[21]</a></sup> His cousin Caroline, with whom he was in love, was married on 27 April 1847, to &#201;mile Dezaunay, a man of 40, with whom she would have five children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMartin1973_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMartin1973-23">[22]</a></sup>\n</p><p>After a short stay in Paris, where he passed first-year law exams, Verne returned to Nantes for his father\'s help in preparing for the second year (provincial law students were in that era required to go to Paris to take exams).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEComp&#232;re1997c41_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEComp&#232;re1997c41-24">[23]</a></sup> While in Nantes, he met Rose Herminie Arnaud Grosseti&#232;re, a young woman one year his senior, and fell intensely in love with her. He wrote and dedicated some 30 poems to her, including "La Fille de l\'air" ("The Daughter of Air"), which describes her as "blonde and enchanting / winged and transparent".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELottmann199614&#8211;15_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELottmann199614&#8211;15-25">[24]</a></sup> His passion seems to have been reciprocated, at least for a short time,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELottmann199614_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELottmann199614-22">[21]</a></sup> but Grosseti&#232;re\'s parents frowned upon the idea of their daughter marrying a young student of uncertain future. They married her instead to Armand Terrien de la Haye, a rich landowner 10 years her senior, on 19 July 1848.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMartin1974_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMartin1974-26">[25]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The sudden marriage sent Verne into deep frustration. He wrote a hallucinatory letter to his mother, apparently composed in a state of half-drunkenness, in which under pretext of a dream he described his misery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELottmann199624_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELottmann199624-27">[26]</a></sup> This requited but aborted love affair seems to have permanently marked the author and his work, and his novels include a significant number of young women married against their will (G&#233;rande in "<a href="/wiki/Master_Zacharius" title="Master Zacharius">Master Zacharius</a>" (1854), Sava in <i><a href="/wiki/Mathias_Sandorf" title="Mathias Sandorf">Mathias Sandorf</a></i> (1885), Ellen in <i><a href="/wiki/A_Floating_City" title="A Floating City">A Floating City</a></i> (1871), etc.), to such an extent that the scholar Christian Chelebourg attributed the recurring theme to a "Herminie complex".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChelebourg1986_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChelebourg1986-28">[27]</a></sup> The incident also led Verne to bear a grudge against his birthplace and Nantes society, which he criticized in his poem "La sixi&#232;me ville de France" ("The Sixth City of France").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELottmann199616_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELottmann199616-29">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVerne2000_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVerne2000-30">[29]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Studies_in_Paris">Studies in Paris</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jules_Verne&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Studies in Paris">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In July 1848, Verne left Nantes again for Paris, where his father intended him to finish law studies and take up law as a profession. He obtained permission from his father to rent a furnished apartment at 24 Rue de l\'Ancienne-Com&#233;die, which he shared with &#201;douard Bonamy, another student of Nantes origin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELottmann199624_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELottmann199624-27">[26]</a></sup> (On his 1847 Paris visit, Verne had stayed at 2 Rue Th&#233;r&#232;se, the house of his aunt Charuel, on the Butte Saint-Roch.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEComp&#232;re1997c42_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEComp&#232;re1997c42-31">[30]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Verne arrived in Paris during a time of political upheaval: the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution_of_1848" title="French Revolution of 1848">French Revolution of 1848</a>. In February, <a href="/wiki/Louis_Philippe_I" title="Louis Philippe I">Louis Philippe I</a> had been overthrown and had fled; on 24 February, a provisional government of the <a href="/wiki/French_Second_Republic" title="French Second Republic">French Second Republic</a> took power, but political demonstrations continued, and social tension remained. In June, barricades went up in Paris, and the government sent <a href="/wiki/Louis-Eug%C3%A8ne_Cavaignac" title="Louis-Eug&#232;ne Cavaignac">Louis-Eug&#232;ne Cavaignac</a> to crush the insurrection. Verne entered the city shortly before the election of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Napol%C3%A9on_III" title="Napol&#233;on III">Louis-Napol&#233;on Bonaparte</a> as the first president of the Republic, a state of affairs that would last until the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/French_coup_of_1851" title="French coup of 1851">French coup of 1851</a> . In a letter to his family, Verne described the bombarded state of the city after the recent <a href="/wiki/June_Days_uprising" title="June Days uprising">June Days uprising</a> but assured them that the anniversary of <a href="/wiki/Bastille_Day" title="Bastille Day">Bastille Day</a> had gone by without any significant conflict.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJules-Verne197612_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJules-Verne197612-32">[31]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Aristide_Hignard_1880.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="673" data-file-width="431" decoding="async" height="234" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Aristide_Hignard_1880.jpg/150px-Aristide_Hignard_1880.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Aristide_Hignard_1880.jpg/225px-Aristide_Hignard_1880.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Aristide_Hignard_1880.jpg/300px-Aristide_Hignard_1880.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Aristide_Hignard_1880.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Aristide Hignard</div></div></div>\n<p>Verne used his family connections to make an entrance into Paris society. His uncle Francisque de Chat&#234;aubourg introduced him into <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Literary_salon" title="Literary salon">literary salons</a>, and Verne particularly frequented those of Mme de Barr&#232;re, a friend of his mother\'s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJules-Verne197617_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJules-Verne197617-33">[32]</a></sup> While continuing his law studies, he fed his passion for the theatre, writing numerous plays. Verne later recalled: "I was greatly under the influence of <a href="/wiki/Victor_Hugo" title="Victor Hugo">Victor Hugo</a>, indeed, very excited by reading and re-reading his works. At that time I could have recited by heart whole pages of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Hunchback_of_Notre-Dame" title="The Hunchback of Notre-Dame">Notre Dame de Paris</a></i>, but it was his dramatic work that most influenced me."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESherard1894&#167;3_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESherard1894&#167;3-34">[33]</a></sup> Another source of creative stimulation came from a neighbor: living on the same floor in the Rue de l\'Ancienne-Com&#233;die apartment house was a young composer, <a href="/wiki/Aristide_Hignard" title="Aristide Hignard">Aristide Hignard</a>, with whom Verne soon became good friends, and Verne wrote several texts for Hignard to set as <a href="/wiki/Chanson" title="Chanson">chansons</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELottmann199632_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELottmann199632-35">[34]</a></sup>\n</p><p>During this period, Verne\'s letters to his parents primarily focused on expenses and on a suddenly appearing series of violent <a href="/wiki/Abdominal_pain" title="Abdominal pain">stomach cramps</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELottmann199625_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELottmann199625-36">[35]</a></sup> the first of many he would suffer from during his life. (Modern scholars have hypothesized that he suffered from <a href="/wiki/Colitis" title="Colitis">colitis</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELottmann199625_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELottmann199625-36">[35]</a></sup> Verne believed the illness to have been inherited from his mother\'s side.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDumas1988372_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDumas1988372-37">[36]</a></sup> Rumors of an outbreak of <a href="/wiki/Cholera" title="Cholera">cholera</a> in March 1849 exacerbated these medical concerns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELottmann199625_36-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELottmann199625-36">[35]</a></sup> Yet another health problem would strike in 1851, when Verne suffered the first of four attacks of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Facial_paralysis" title="Facial paralysis">facial paralysis</a>. These attacks, rather than being <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Psychosomatic" title="Psychosomatic">psychosomatic</a>, were due to an inflammation in the <a href="/wiki/Middle_ear" title="Middle ear">middle ear</a>, though this cause remained unknown to Verne during his life.\n<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDumas200051_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDumas200051-38">[37]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the same year, Verne was required to enlist in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/French_military" title="French military">French military</a>, but the <a href="/wiki/Sortition" title="Sortition">sortition</a> process spared him, to his great relief. He wrote to his father: "You should already know, dear papa, what I think of the military life, and of these domestic servants in livery. &#8230; You have to abandon all dignity to perform such functions."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELottmann199629_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELottmann199629-39">[38]</a></sup> Verne\'s strong antiwar sentiments, to the dismay of his father, would remain steadfast throughout his life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELottmann199629_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELottmann199629-39">[38]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Though writing profusely and frequenting the salons, Verne diligently pursued his law studies and graduated with a <i>licence en droit</i> in January 1851.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans198817_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans198817-40">[39]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Literary_debut">Literary debut</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jules_Verne&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Literary debut">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Thanks to his visits to salons, Verne came into contact in 1849 with <a href="/wiki/Alexandre_Dumas" title="Alexandre Dumas">Alexandre Dumas</a> through the mutual acquaintance of a celebrated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chirologist" title="Chirologist">chirologist</a> of the time, the Chevalier d\'Arpentigny.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans198817_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans198817-40">[39]</a></sup> Verne became close friends with Dumas\' son, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alexandre_Dumas,_fils" title="Alexandre Dumas, fils">Alexandre Dumas, fils</a>, and showed him a manuscript for a stage comedy, <i>Les Pailles rompues</i> (<i>The Broken Straws</i>). The two young men revised the play together, and Dumas, through arrangements with his father, had it produced by the <a href="/wiki/Op%C3%A9ra-National" title="Op&#233;ra-National">Op&#233;ra-National</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Th%C3%A9%C3%A2tre_Historique" title="Th&#233;&#226;tre Historique">Th&#233;&#226;tre Historique</a> in Paris, opening on 12 June 1850.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDekissDehs199929_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDekissDehs199929-41">[40]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Le_Mus%C3%A9e_des_familles_1854-1855.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="355" data-file-width="224" decoding="async" height="238" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Le_Mus%C3%A9e_des_familles_1854-1855.jpg/150px-Le_Mus%C3%A9e_des_familles_1854-1855.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/19/Le_Mus%C3%A9e_des_familles_1854-1855.jpg 1.5x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Le_Mus%C3%A9e_des_familles_1854-1855.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cover of an 1854&#8211;55 issue of <i>Mus&#233;e des familles</i></div></div></div>\n<p>In 1851, Verne met with a fellow writer from Nantes, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pierre-Michel-Fran%C3%A7ois_Chevalier" title="Pierre-Michel-Fran&#231;ois Chevalier">Pierre-Michel-Fran&#231;ois Chevalier</a> (known as "Pitre-Chevalier"), the editor-in-chief of the magazine <i><a href="/wiki/Mus%C3%A9e_des_familles" title="Mus&#233;e des familles">Mus&#233;e des familles</a></i> (<i>The Family Museum</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELottmann199637_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELottmann199637-42">[41]</a></sup> Pitre-Chevalier was looking for articles about geography, history, science, and technology, and was keen to make sure that the educational component would be made accessible to large popular audiences using a straightforward prose style or an engaging fictional story. Verne, with his delight in diligent research, especially in geography, was a natural for the job.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans198818_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans198818-43">[42]</a></sup> Verne first offered him a short <a href="/wiki/Historical_fiction" title="Historical fiction">historical</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adventure_story" title="Adventure story">adventure story</a>, "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_First_Ships_of_the_Mexican_Navy" title="The First Ships of the Mexican Navy">The First Ships of the Mexican Navy</a>", written in the style of <a href="/wiki/James_Fenimore_Cooper" title="James Fenimore Cooper">James Fenimore Cooper</a>, whose novels had deeply influenced him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELottmann199637_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELottmann199637-42">[41]</a></sup> Pitre-Chevalier published it in July 1851, and in the same year published a second short story, "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/A_Voyage_in_a_Balloon" title="A Voyage in a Balloon">A Voyage in a Balloon</a>"(August 1851). The latter story, with its combination of adventurous narrative, travel themes, and detailed historical research, would later be described by Verne as "the first indication of the line of novel that I was destined to follow."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESherard1894&#167;3_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESherard1894&#167;3-34">[33]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Dumas fils put Verne in contact with Jules Seveste, a stage director who had taken over the directorship of the Th&#233;&#226;tre-Historique and renamed it the <a href="/wiki/Th%C3%A9%C3%A2tre_Lyrique" title="Th&#233;&#226;tre Lyrique">Th&#233;&#226;tre Lyrique</a>. Seveste offered Verne the job of secretary of the theatre, with little or no salary attached.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEButcher2007_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEButcher2007-8">[8]</a></sup> Verne accepted, using the opportunity to write and produce several comic operas written in collaboration with Hignard and the prolific librettist <a href="/wiki/Michel_Carr%C3%A9" title="Michel Carr&#233;">Michel Carr&#233;</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELottmann199653,_58_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELottmann199653,_58-44">[43]</a></sup> To celebrate his employment at the Th&#233;&#226;tre Lyrique, Verne joined with ten friends to found a bachelors\' dining club, the <i>Onze-sans-femme</i> (Eleven Bachelors).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJules-Verne197627_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJules-Verne197627-45">[44]</a></sup>\n</p><p>For some time, Verne\'s father pressed him to abandon his writing and begin a business as a lawyer. However, Verne argued in his letters that he could only find success in literature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELottmann199638_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELottmann199638-46">[45]</a></sup> The pressure to plan for a secure future in law reached its climax in January 1852, when his father offered Verne his own Nantes law practice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELottmann199646&#8211;47_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELottmann199646&#8211;47-47">[46]</a></sup> Faced with this ultimatum, Verne decided conclusively to continue his literary life and refuse the job, writing: "Am I not right to follow my own instincts? It\'s because I know who I am that I realize what I can be one day."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELottmann199647_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELottmann199647-48">[47]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Arago,_Jacques_(colored).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="581" data-file-width="491" decoding="async" height="177" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Arago%2C_Jacques_%28colored%29.jpg/150px-Arago%2C_Jacques_%28colored%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Arago%2C_Jacques_%28colored%29.jpg/225px-Arago%2C_Jacques_%28colored%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Arago%2C_Jacques_%28colored%29.jpg/300px-Arago%2C_Jacques_%28colored%29.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Arago,_Jacques_(colored).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Jacques Arago</div></div></div>\n<p>Meanwhile, Verne was spending much time at the <a href="/wiki/Biblioth%C3%A8que_nationale_de_France" title="Biblioth&#232;que nationale de France">Biblioth&#232;que nationale de France</a>, conducting research for his stories and feeding his passion for science and recent discoveries, especially in <a href="/wiki/Geography" title="Geography">geography</a>. It was in this period that Verne met the illustrious geographer and explorer <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Arago" title="Jacques Arago">Jacques Arago</a>, who continued to travel extensively despite his blindness (he had lost his sight completely in 1837). The two men became good friends, and Arago\'s innovative and witty accounts of his travels led Verne toward a newly developing genre of literature: that of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Travel_writing" title="Travel writing">travel writing</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDekissDehs199930&#8211;31_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDekissDehs199930&#8211;31-49">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELottmann199639&#8211;40_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELottmann199639&#8211;40-50">[49]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1852, two new pieces from Verne appeared in the <i>Mus&#233;e des familles</i>: "<a href="/wiki/Martin_Paz" title="Martin Paz">Martin Paz</a>", a novella set in Lima, which Verne wrote in 1851 and published 10 July through 11 August 1852, and <i>Les Ch&#226;teaux en Californie, ou, Pierre qui roule n\'amasse pas mousse</i> (<i>The Castles in California, or, A Rolling Stone Gathers No Moss</i>), a one-act comedy full of racy <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Double_entendres" title="Double entendres">double entendres</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMargot2005151_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMargot2005151-51">[50]</a></sup> In April and May 1854, the magazine published Verne\'s short story "<a href="/wiki/Master_Zacharius" title="Master Zacharius">Master Zacharius</a>", an <a href="/wiki/E._T._A._Hoffmann" title="E. T. A. Hoffmann">E. T. A. Hoffmann</a>-like fantasy featuring a sharp condemnation of scientific <a href="/wiki/Hubris" title="Hubris">hubris</a> and ambition,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELottmann199657_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELottmann199657-52">[51]</a></sup> followed soon afterward by "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/A_Winter_Amid_the_Ice" title="A Winter Amid the Ice">A Winter Amid the Ice</a>", a polar adventure story whose themes closely anticipated many of Verne\'s novels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELottmann199658_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELottmann199658-53">[52]</a></sup> The <i>Mus&#233;e</i> also published some nonfiction <a href="/wiki/Popular_science" title="Popular science">popular science</a> articles which, though unsigned, are generally attributed to Verne.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans198818_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans198818-43">[42]</a></sup> Verne\'s work for the magazine was cut short in 1856, when he had a serious quarrel with Pitre-Chevalier and refused to continue contributing (a refusal he would maintain until 1863, when Pitre-Chevalier died, and the magazine went to new editorship).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans198819_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans198819-54">[53]</a></sup>\n</p><p>While writing stories and articles for Pitre-Chevalier, Verne began to form the idea of inventing a new kind of novel, a <i>Roman de la Science</i> (novel of science), which would allow him to incorporate large amounts of the factual information he so enjoyed researching in the Biblioth&#232;que. He is said to have discussed the project with the elder Alexandre Dumas, who had tried something similar with an unfinished novel, <i>Isaac Laquedem</i>, and who enthusiastically encouraged Verne\'s project.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans198818&#8211;19_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans198818&#8211;19-55">[54]</a></sup>\n</p><p>At the end of 1854, another outbreak of cholera led to the death of Jules Seveste, Verne\'s employer at the Th&#233;&#226;tre Lyrique and by then a good friend.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELottmann199658_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELottmann199658-53">[52]</a></sup> Though his contract only held him to a further year of service, Verne remained connected to the theatre for several years after Seveste\'s death, seeing additional productions to fruition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJules-Verne197637_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJules-Verne197637-56">[55]</a></sup> He also continued to write plays and musical comedies, most of which were not performed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans198819_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans198819-54">[53]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Family">Family</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jules_Verne&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Family">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In May 1856, Verne traveled to <a href="/wiki/Amiens" title="Amiens">Amiens</a> to be the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Best_man" title="Best man">best man</a> at the wedding of a Nantes friend, Auguste Lelarge, to an Amiens woman named Aim&#233;e du Fraysse de Viane. Verne, invited to stay with the bride\'s family, took to them warmly, befriending the entire household and finding himself increasingly attracted to the bride\'s sister, Honorine de Viane Morel, a widow aged 26 with two young children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJules-Verne197640&#8211;41_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJules-Verne197640&#8211;41-57">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELottmann199666&#8211;67_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELottmann199666&#8211;67-58">[57]</a></sup> Hoping to find a secure source of income, as well as a chance to court Morel in earnest, he jumped at her brother\'s offer to go into business with a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brokerage" title="Brokerage">brokerage</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJules-Verne197642&#8211;43_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJules-Verne197642&#8211;43-59">[58]</a></sup> Verne\'s father was initially dubious but gave in to his son\'s requests for approval in November 1856. With his financial situation finally looking promising, Verne won the favor of Morel and her family, and the couple were married on 10 January 1857.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJules-Verne197644_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJules-Verne197644-60">[59]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Verne plunged into his new business obligations, leaving his work at the Th&#233;&#226;tre Lyrique and taking up a full-time job as an <i>agent de change</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans198819_54-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans198819-54">[53]</a></sup> on the <a href="/wiki/Paris_Bourse" title="Paris Bourse">Paris Bourse</a>, where he became the associate of the broker Fernand Eggly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELottmann199676&#8211;78_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELottmann199676&#8211;78-61">[60]</a></sup> Verne woke up early each morning so that he would have time to write, before going to the Bourse for the day\'s work; in the rest of his spare time, he continued to consort with the <i>Onze-Sans-Femme</i> club (all eleven of its "bachelors" had by this time gotten married). He also continued to frequent the Biblioth&#232;que to do scientific and historical research, much of which he copied onto notecards for future use&#8212;a system he would continue for the rest of his life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans198819_54-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans198819-54">[53]</a></sup> According to the recollections of a colleague, Verne "did better in repartee than in business".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELottmann199676&#8211;78_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELottmann199676&#8211;78-61">[60]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jules_Verne_Funeral_Procession_1905.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="830" data-file-width="1185" decoding="async" height="154" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Jules_Verne_Funeral_Procession_1905.jpg/220px-Jules_Verne_Funeral_Procession_1905.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Jules_Verne_Funeral_Procession_1905.jpg/330px-Jules_Verne_Funeral_Procession_1905.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Jules_Verne_Funeral_Procession_1905.jpg/440px-Jules_Verne_Funeral_Procession_1905.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jules_Verne_Funeral_Procession_1905.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Jules Verne Funeral Procession, headed by his son and grandson, 1905</div></div></div>\n<p>In July 1858, Verne and Aristide Hignard seized an opportunity offered by Hignard\'s brother: a sea voyage, at no charge, from <a href="/wiki/Bordeaux" title="Bordeaux">Bordeaux</a> to <a href="/wiki/Liverpool" title="Liverpool">Liverpool</a> and Scotland. The journey, Verne\'s first trip outside France, deeply impressed him, and upon his return to Paris he fictionalized his recollections to form the backbone of a semi-autobiographical novel, <i><a href="/wiki/Backwards_to_Britain" title="Backwards to Britain">Backwards to Britain</a></i> (written in the autumn and winter of 1859&#8211;1860 and not published until 1989).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELottmann199679_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELottmann199679-62">[61]</a></sup> A second complementary voyage in 1861 took Hignard and Verne to <a href="/wiki/Stockholm" title="Stockholm">Stockholm</a>, from where they traveled to <a href="/wiki/Oslo" title="Oslo">Christiania</a> and through <a href="/wiki/Telemark" title="Telemark">Telemark</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELottmann199681_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELottmann199681-63">[62]</a></sup> Verne left Hignard in Denmark to return in haste to Paris, but missed the birth on 3 August 1861 of his only biological son, <a href="/wiki/Michel_Verne" title="Michel Verne">Michel</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJules-Verne197654_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJules-Verne197654-64">[63]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Meanwhile, Verne continued work on the idea of a <i>Roman de la Science</i>, which he developed in a rough draft inspired, according to his recollections, by his "love for maps and the great explorers of the world". It took shape as a story of travel across Africa and would eventually become his first published novel, <i><a href="/wiki/Five_Weeks_in_a_Balloon" title="Five Weeks in a Balloon">Five Weeks in a Balloon</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans198819_54-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans198819-54">[53]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hetzel">Hetzel</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jules_Verne&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Hetzel">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pierre-Jules_Hetzel.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1219" data-file-width="783" decoding="async" height="234" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Pierre-Jules_Hetzel.jpg/150px-Pierre-Jules_Hetzel.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Pierre-Jules_Hetzel.jpg/225px-Pierre-Jules_Hetzel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Pierre-Jules_Hetzel.jpg/300px-Pierre-Jules_Hetzel.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pierre-Jules_Hetzel.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Pierre-Jules Hetzel</div></div></div>\n<p>In 1862, through their mutual acquaintance Alfred de Br&#233;hat, Verne came into contact with the publisher <a href="/wiki/Pierre-Jules_Hetzel" title="Pierre-Jules Hetzel">Pierre-Jules Hetzel</a>, and submitted to him the manuscript of his developing novel, then called <i>Voyage en Ballon</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJules-Verne197654&#8211;55_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJules-Verne197654&#8211;55-65">[64]</a></sup> Hetzel, already the publisher of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Balzac" title="Balzac">Balzac</a>, <a href="/wiki/George_Sand" title="George Sand">George Sand</a>, <a href="/wiki/Victor_Hugo" title="Victor Hugo">Victor Hugo</a>, and other well-known authors, had long been planning to launch a high-quality family magazine in which entertaining fiction would combine with scientific education.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans198823&#8211;24_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans198823&#8211;24-66">[65]</a></sup> He saw Verne, with his demonstrated inclination toward scrupulously researched adventure stories, as an ideal contributor for such a magazine, and accepted the novel, giving Verne suggestions for improvement. Verne made the proposed revisions within two weeks and returned to Hetzel with the final draft, now titled <i><a href="/wiki/Five_Weeks_in_a_Balloon" title="Five Weeks in a Balloon">Five Weeks in a Balloon</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJules-Verne197656_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJules-Verne197656-67">[66]</a></sup> It was published by Hetzel on 31 January 1863.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-biblioVE_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-biblioVE-68">[67]</a></sup>\n</p><p>To secure his services for the planned magazine, to be called the <i>Magasin d\'&#201;ducation et de R&#233;cr&#233;ation</i> (<i>Magazine of Education and Recreation</i>), Hetzel also drew up a long-term contract in which Verne would give him three volumes of text per year, each of which Hetzel would buy outright for a flat fee. Verne, finding both a steady salary and a sure outlet for writing at last, accepted immediately.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJules-Verne197656&#8211;57_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJules-Verne197656&#8211;57-69">[68]</a></sup> For the rest of his lifetime, most of his novels would be serialized in Hetzel\'s <i>Magasin</i> before their appearance in book form, beginning with his second novel for Hetzel, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Adventures_of_Captain_Hatteras" title="The Adventures of Captain Hatteras">The Adventures of Captain Hatteras</a></i> (1864&#8211;65).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-biblioVE_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-biblioVE-68">[67]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hetzel_front_cover.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="827" data-file-width="545" decoding="async" height="334" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Hetzel_front_cover.jpg/220px-Hetzel_front_cover.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Hetzel_front_cover.jpg/330px-Hetzel_front_cover.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Hetzel_front_cover.jpg/440px-Hetzel_front_cover.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hetzel_front_cover.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Hetzel edition of Verne\'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Adventures_of_Captain_Hatteras" title="The Adventures of Captain Hatteras">The Adventures of Captain Hatteras</a></i> (cover style "Aux deux &#233;l&#233;phants")</div></div></div>\n<p>When <i>The Adventures of Captain Hatteras</i> was published in book form in 1866, Hetzel publicly announced his literary and educational ambitions for Verne\'s novels by saying in a preface that Verne\'s works would form a <a href="/wiki/Novel_sequence" title="Novel sequence">novel sequence</a> called the <i><a href="/wiki/Voyages_extraordinaires" title="Voyages extraordinaires">Voyages extraordinaires</a></i> (<i>Extraordinary Voyages</i> or <i>Extraordinary Journeys</i>), and that Verne\'s aim was "to outline all the geographical, geological, physical, and astronomical knowledge amassed by modern science and to recount, in an entertaining and picturesque format that is his own, the history of the universe."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans198829&#8211;30_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans198829&#8211;30-70">[69]</a></sup> Late in life, Verne confirmed that this commission had become the running theme of his novels: "My object has been to depict the earth, and not the earth alone, but the universe&#8230; And I have tried at the same time to realize a very high ideal of beauty of style. It is said that there can\'t be any style in a novel of adventure, but it isn\'t true."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESherard1894&#167;4_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESherard1894&#167;4-71">[70]</a></sup> However, he also noted that the project was extremely ambitious: "Yes! But the Earth is very large, and life is very short! In order to leave a completed work behind, one would need to live to be at least 100 years old!"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans198830_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans198830-72">[71]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Hetzel influenced many of Verne\'s novels directly, especially in the first few years of their collaboration, for Verne was initially so happy to find a publisher that he agreed to almost all of the changes Hetzel suggested. For example, when Hetzel disapproved of the original climax of <i>Captain Hatteras</i>, including the death of the title character, Verne wrote an entirely new conclusion in which Hatteras survived.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans200198&#8211;99_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans200198&#8211;99-73">[72]</a></sup> Hetzel also rejected Verne\'s next submission, <i><a href="/wiki/Paris_in_the_Twentieth_Century" title="Paris in the Twentieth Century">Paris in the Twentieth Century</a></i>, believing its pessimistic view of the future and its condemnation of technological progress were too subversive for a family magazine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELottmann1996101&#8211;103_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELottmann1996101&#8211;103-74">[73]</a></sup> (The manuscript, believed <a href="/wiki/Lost_work" title="Lost work">lost</a> for some time after Verne\'s death, was finally published in 1994.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans199544_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans199544-75">[74]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The relationship between publisher and writer changed significantly around 1869, when Verne and Hetzel were brought into conflict over the manuscript for <i><a href="/wiki/Twenty_Thousand_Leagues_Under_the_Sea" title="Twenty Thousand Leagues Under the Sea">Twenty Thousand Leagues Under the Sea</a></i>. Verne had initially conceived of the submariner <a href="/wiki/Captain_Nemo" title="Captain Nemo">Captain Nemo</a> as a Polish scientist whose acts of vengeance were directed against the Russians who had killed his family during the <a href="/wiki/January_Uprising" title="January Uprising">January uprising</a>. Hetzel, not wanting to alienate the lucrative Russian market for Verne\'s books, demanded that Nemo be made an enemy of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slave_trade" title="Slave trade">slave trade</a>, a situation that would make him an unambiguous hero. Verne, after fighting vehemently against the change, finally invented a compromise in which Nemo\'s past is left mysterious. After this disagreement, Verne became notably cooler in his dealings with Hetzel, taking suggestions into consideration but often rejecting them outright.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans2001100&#8211;101_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans2001100&#8211;101-76">[75]</a></sup>\n</p><p>From that point, Verne published two or more volumes a year. The most successful of these are: <i>Voyage au centre de la Terre</i> (<i><a href="/wiki/Journey_to_the_Center_of_the_Earth" title="Journey to the Center of the Earth">Journey to the Center of the Earth</a></i>, 1864); <i>De la Terre &#224; la Lune</i> (<i><a href="/wiki/From_the_Earth_to_the_Moon" title="From the Earth to the Moon">From the Earth to the Moon</a></i>, 1865); <i>Vingt mille lieues sous les mers</i> (<i><a href="/wiki/Twenty_Thousand_Leagues_Under_the_Sea" title="Twenty Thousand Leagues Under the Sea">Twenty Thousand Leagues Under the Sea</a></i>, 1869); and <i>Le tour du monde en quatre-vingts jours</i> (<i><a href="/wiki/Around_the_World_in_Eighty_Days" title="Around the World in Eighty Days">Around the World in Eighty Days</a></i>), which first appeared in <i>Le Temps</i> in 1872. Verne could now live on his writings. But most of his wealth came from the stage adaptations of <i>Le tour du monde en quatre-vingts jours</i> (1874) and <i>Michel Strogoff</i> (1876), which he wrote with <a href="/wiki/Adolphe_d%27Ennery" title="Adolphe d\'Ennery">Adolphe d\'Ennery</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Saint-Michel_sketch.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1552" data-file-width="1891" decoding="async" height="181" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Saint-Michel_sketch.jpg/220px-Saint-Michel_sketch.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Saint-Michel_sketch.jpg/330px-Saint-Michel_sketch.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Saint-Michel_sketch.jpg/440px-Saint-Michel_sketch.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Saint-Michel_sketch.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sketch by Verne of the <i>Saint-Michel</i></div></div></div>\n<p>In 1867, Verne bought a small ship, the <i>Saint-Michel</i>, which he successively replaced with the <i>Saint-Michel II</i> and the <i>Saint-Michel III</i> as his financial situation improved. On board the <i>Saint-Michel III</i>, he sailed around Europe.  After his first novel, most of his stories were first serialised in the <i>Magazine d\'&#201;ducation et de R&#233;cr&#233;ation</i>, a Hetzel biweekly publication, before being published in book form. His brother Paul contributed to <i>40th French climbing of the Mont-Blanc</i> and a collection of short stories &#8211; <i>Doctor Ox</i> &#8211; in 1874. Verne became wealthy and famous.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p><p>Meanwhile, Michel Verne married an actress against his father\'s wishes, had two children by an underage mistress, and buried himself in debts.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The relationship between father and son improved as Michel grew older.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Later_years">Later years</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jules_Verne&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Later years">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Though he was raised Catholic, Verne became a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deist" title="Deist">deist</a> in his later years, from about 1870 onward.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJules-Verne19769_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJules-Verne19769-77">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECostello197834_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECostello197834-78">[77]</a></sup> Some scholars believe his deist philosophy is reflected in his novels, as they often involve the notion of God or <a href="/wiki/Divine_providence" title="Divine providence">divine providence</a> but rarely mention the concept of Christ.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVerne2007412_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVerne2007412-79">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOliver201222_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOliver201222-80">[79]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jules_Verne_and_Mrs._Verne_ca.1900.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1348" data-file-width="1158" decoding="async" height="256" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Jules_Verne_and_Mrs._Verne_ca.1900.jpg/220px-Jules_Verne_and_Mrs._Verne_ca.1900.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Jules_Verne_and_Mrs._Verne_ca.1900.jpg/330px-Jules_Verne_and_Mrs._Verne_ca.1900.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Jules_Verne_and_Mrs._Verne_ca.1900.jpg/440px-Jules_Verne_and_Mrs._Verne_ca.1900.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jules_Verne_and_Mrs._Verne_ca.1900.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Jules Verne and Madame Verne ca. 1900</div></div></div>\n<p>On 9 March 1886, as Verne was coming home, his twenty-six-year-old nephew, Gaston, shot at him twice with a <a href="/wiki/Pistol" title="Pistol">pistol</a>. The first bullet missed, but the second one entered Verne\'s left leg, giving him a permanent limp that could not be overcome. This incident was hushed up in the media, but Gaston spent the rest of his life in a <a href="/wiki/Lunatic_asylum" title="Lunatic asylum">mental asylum</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[80]</a></sup>\n</p><p>After the death of both his mother and Hetzel, Jules Verne began publishing darker works. In 1888, Verne entered politics and was elected town councilor of <a href="/wiki/Amiens" title="Amiens">Amiens</a>, where he championed several improvements and served for fifteen years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[81]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Verne was made a Chevalier de la <a href="/wiki/Legion_of_Honour" title="Legion of Honour">L&#233;gion d\'honneur</a> in 1870. He was promoted to an Officier de la L&#233;gion d\'honneur in 1892.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Death_and_posthumous_publications">Death and posthumous publications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jules_Verne&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Death and posthumous publications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jules_Verne_on_his_deathbed.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1645" data-file-width="2609" decoding="async" height="139" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Jules_Verne_on_his_deathbed.jpg/220px-Jules_Verne_on_his_deathbed.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Jules_Verne_on_his_deathbed.jpg/330px-Jules_Verne_on_his_deathbed.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Jules_Verne_on_his_deathbed.jpg/440px-Jules_Verne_on_his_deathbed.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jules_Verne_on_his_deathbed.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Jules Verne on his deathbed, 1905</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Verne_tomb.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="537" decoding="async" height="223" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/5/5b/Verne_tomb.jpg/150px-Verne_tomb.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/5/5b/Verne_tomb.jpg/225px-Verne_tomb.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/5/5b/Verne_tomb.jpg/300px-Verne_tomb.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Verne_tomb.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Verne\'s tomb in Amiens</div></div></div>\n<p>On 24 March 1905, while ill with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diabetes" title="Diabetes">diabetes</a>, Verne died at his home in <a href="/wiki/Amiens" title="Amiens">Amiens</a>, 44 Boulevard Longueville (now Boulevard Jules-Verne). His son, Michel Verne, oversaw publication of the novels <i>Invasion of the Sea</i> and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Le_Phare_du_bout_du_monde" title="Le Phare du bout du monde">The Lighthouse at the End of the World</a></i> after Jules\'s death. The <i>Voyages extraordinaires</i> series continued for several years afterwards at the same rate of two volumes a year. It was later discovered that Michel Verne had made extensive changes in these stories, and the original versions were eventually published at the end of the 20th century by the Jules Verne Society (Soci&#233;t&#233; Jules Verne)<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>. In 1919, Michel Verne published <a href="/wiki/The_Barsac_Mission" title="The Barsac Mission">The Barsac Mission</a> (<a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a>: <i lang="fr">L\'&#201;tonnante Aventure de la Mission Barsac</i>), which original drafts contained references to <a href="/wiki/Esperanto" title="Esperanto">Esperanto</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[82]</a></sup> language about which his father had great interest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[84]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1989, Verne\'s great-grandson discovered his ancestor\'s as yet unpublished novel <i><a href="/wiki/Paris_in_the_Twentieth_Century" title="Paris in the Twentieth Century">Paris in the Twentieth Century</a></i> which was subsequently published in 1994.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Works">Works</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jules_Verne&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Works">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:RO025MS-05.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="490" data-file-width="500" decoding="async" height="216" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/RO025MS-05.jpg/220px-RO025MS-05.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/RO025MS-05.jpg/330px-RO025MS-05.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/RO025MS-05.jpg/440px-RO025MS-05.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:RO025MS-05.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Jules Verne novels: <a href="/wiki/The_Carpathian_Castle" title="The Carpathian Castle">The Carpathian Castle</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Danube_Pilot" title="The Danube Pilot">The Danube Pilot</a>, <a href="/wiki/Claudius_Bombarnac" title="Claudius Bombarnac">Claudius Bombarnac</a> and <a href="/wiki/K%C3%A9raban_the_Inflexible" title="K&#233;raban the Inflexible">K&#233;raban the Inflexible</a>. Miniature sheet, post of Romania 2005.</div></div></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Jules_Verne_bibliography" title="Jules Verne bibliography">Jules Verne bibliography</a></div>\n<p>Verne\'s largest body of work is the <i><a href="/wiki/Voyages_extraordinaires" title="Voyages extraordinaires">Voyages extraordinaires</a></i> series, which includes all of his novels except for the two rejected manuscripts <i><a href="/wiki/Paris_in_the_Twentieth_Century" title="Paris in the Twentieth Century">Paris in the Twentieth Century</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Backwards_to_Britain" title="Backwards to Britain">Backwards to Britain</a></i> (published posthumously in 1994 and 1989, respectively) and for projects left unfinished at his death (many of which would be posthumously adapted or rewritten for publication by his son Michel).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDehsMargotHar\'El2007[httpjvgileadorgilbiblioapocryphahtml_X]_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDehsMargotHar\'El2007[httpjvgileadorgilbiblioapocryphahtml_X]-86">[85]</a></sup> Verne also wrote many plays, poems, song texts, operetta libretti, and short stories, as well as a variety of essays and miscellaneous non-fiction.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Literary_reception">Literary reception</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jules_Verne&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Literary reception">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1889_Verne_poster.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="550" data-file-width="409" decoding="async" height="296" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/1889_Verne_poster.jpg/220px-1889_Verne_poster.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/1889_Verne_poster.jpg/330px-1889_Verne_poster.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b5/1889_Verne_poster.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1889_Verne_poster.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An 1889 Hetzel poster advertising Verne\'s works</div></div></div>\n<p>After his <a href="/wiki/Debut_novel" title="Debut novel">debut</a> under Hetzel, Verne was enthusiastically received in France by writers and scientists alike, with <a href="/wiki/George_Sand" title="George Sand">George Sand</a> and <a href="/wiki/Th%C3%A9ophile_Gautier" title="Th&#233;ophile Gautier">Th&#233;ophile Gautier</a> among his earliest admirers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans200011&#8211;12_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans200011&#8211;12-87">[86]</a></sup> Several notable contemporary figures, from the geographer Vivien de Saint-Martin to the critic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jules_Claretie" title="Jules Claretie">Jules Claretie</a>, spoke highly of Verne and his works in critical and biographical notes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans200012&#8211;13_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans200012&#8211;13-88">[87]</a></sup>\n</p><p>However, Verne\'s growing popularity among readers and playgoers (due especially to the highly successful stage version of <i>Around the World in Eighty Days</i>) led to a gradual change in his literary reputation.  As the novels and stage productions continued to sell, many contemporary critics felt that Verne\'s status as a commercially popular author meant he could only be seen as a mere genre-based storyteller, rather than a serious author worthy of academic study.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans200014_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans200014-89">[88]</a></sup>\n</p><p>This denial of formal literary status took various forms, including dismissive criticism by such writers as <a href="/wiki/%C3%89mile_Zola" title="&#201;mile Zola">&#201;mile Zola</a> and the lack of nomination to Verne for membership in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acad%C3%A9mie_Fran%C3%A7aise" title="Acad&#233;mie Fran&#231;aise">Acad&#233;mie Fran&#231;aise</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans200014_89-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans200014-89">[88]</a></sup> and was recognized by Verne himself, who said in a late interview: "The great regret of my life is that I have never taken any place in French literature."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESherard1894&#167;1_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESherard1894&#167;1-90">[89]</a></sup> To Verne, who considered himself "a man of letters and an artist, living in the pursuit of the ideal",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESherard1894&#167;6_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESherard1894&#167;6-91">[90]</a></sup> this critical dismissal on the basis of literary ideology could only be seen as the ultimate snub.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans200015_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans200015-92">[91]</a></sup>\n</p><p>This bifurcation of Verne as a popular genre writer but a critical <i><a href="/wiki/Persona_non_grata" title="Persona non grata">persona non grata</a></i> continued after his death, with early biographies (including one by Verne\'s own niece, Marguerite Allotte de la Fu&#255;e) focusing on error-filled and embroidered <a href="/wiki/Hagiography" title="Hagiography">hagiography</a> of Verne as a popular figure rather than on Verne\'s actual working methods or his output.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans200022&#8211;23_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans200022&#8211;23-93">[92]</a></sup> Meanwhile, sales of Verne\'s novels in their original unabridged versions dropped markedly even in Verne\'s home country, with abridged versions aimed directly at children taking their place.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans200023_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans200023-94">[93]</a></sup>\n</p><p>However, the decades after Verne\'s death also saw the rise in France of the "Jules Verne cult", a steadily growing group of scholars and young writers who took Verne\'s works seriously as literature and willingly noted his influence on their own pioneering works. Some of the cult founded the Soci&#233;t&#233; Jules Verne, the first academic society for Verne scholars; many others became highly respected <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Avant_garde" title="Avant garde">avant garde</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Surrealist" title="Surrealist">surrealist</a> literary figures in their own right. Their praise and analyses, emphasizing Verne\'s stylistic innovations and enduring literary themes, proved highly influential for literary studies to come.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans200024&#8211;6_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans200024&#8211;6-95">[94]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the 1960s and 1970s, thanks in large part to a sustained wave of serious literary study from well-known French scholars and writers, Verne\'s reputation skyrocketed in France.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAngenot197646_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAngenot197646-96">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans200029_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans200029-97">[96]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Roland_Barthes" title="Roland Barthes">Roland Barthes</a>\' seminal essay "Nautilus et Bateau Ivre" ("The <a href="/wiki/Nautilus_(Verne)" title="Nautilus (Verne)">Nautilus</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Drunken_Boat" title="Drunken Boat">Drunken Boat</a>") was influential in its <a href="/wiki/Exegesis" title="Exegesis">exegesis</a> of the <i>Voyages extraordinares</i> as a purely literary text, while book-length studies by such figures as Marcel Mor&#233; and Jean Chesneaux considered Verne from a multitude of thematic vantage points.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAngenot197335&#8211;36_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAngenot197335&#8211;36-98">[97]</a></sup>\n</p><p>French literary journals devoted entire issues to Verne and his work, with essays by such imposing literary figures as <a href="/wiki/Michel_Butor" title="Michel Butor">Michel Butor</a>, <a href="/wiki/Georges_Borgeaud" title="Georges Borgeaud">Georges Borgeaud</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marcel_Brion" title="Marcel Brion">Marcel Brion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Versins" title="Pierre Versins">Pierre Versins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Michel_Foucault" title="Michel Foucault">Michel Foucault</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Barjavel" title="Ren&#233; Barjavel">Ren&#233; Barjavel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marcel_Lecomte" title="Marcel Lecomte">Marcel Lecomte</a>, <a href="/wiki/Francis_Lacassin" title="Francis Lacassin">Francis Lacassin</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Michel_Serres" title="Michel Serres">Michel Serres</a>; meanwhile, Verne\'s entire published opus returned to print, with unabridged and illustrated editions of his works printed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Livre_de_Poche" title="Livre de Poche">Livre de Poche</a> and &#201;ditions Rencontre.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans200029&#8211;30_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans200029&#8211;30-99">[98]</a></sup> The wave reached its climax in Verne\'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sesquicentennial" title="Sesquicentennial">sesquicentennial</a> year 1978, when he was made the subject of an academic colloquium at the <a href="/wiki/Centre_culturel_international_de_Cerisy-la-Salle" title="Centre culturel international de Cerisy-la-Salle">Centre culturel international de Cerisy-la-Salle</a>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Journey_to_the_Center_of_the_Earth" title="Journey to the Center of the Earth">Journey to the Center of the Earth</a></i> was accepted for the French university system\'s <a href="/wiki/Agr%C3%A9gation" title="Agr&#233;gation">Agr&#233;gation</a> reading list. Since these events, Verne has been consistently recognized in Europe as a legitimate member of the French literary canon, with academic studies and new publications steadily continuing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans200032&#8211;33_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans200032&#8211;33-100">[99]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Verne\'s reputation in English-speaking countries has been considerably slower in changing.
